<p><b>Transsexual</b> people experience a <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a> that is inconsistent with, or not culturally associated with, their <a href="/wiki/Sex_assignment" title="Sex assignment">assigned sex</a>, and desire to permanently <a href="/wiki/Transitioning_(transgender)" title="Transitioning (transgender)">transition</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a> with which they identify, usually seeking medical assistance (including <a href="/wiki/Hormone_replacement_therapy" title="Hormone replacement therapy">hormone replacement therapy</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Sex_reassignment_therapy" title="Sex reassignment therapy">sex reassignment therapies</a>) to help them align their body with their identified <a href="/wiki/Sex" title="Sex">sex</a> or gender. <i>Transsexual</i> is generally considered a subset of <i><a href="/wiki/Transgender" title="Transgender">transgender</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TR2006_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-TR2006-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bevan_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bevan-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alegria_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alegria-3">[3]</a></sup> but some transsexual people reject the label of <i>transgender</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Valentine_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Valentine-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stryker_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stryker-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> A medical diagnosis of <a href="/wiki/Gender_dysphoria" title="Gender dysphoria">gender dysphoria</a> can be made if a person expresses a desire to live and be accepted as a member of their identified sex,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icd-10_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-icd-10-8">[8]</a></sup> or if a person experiences impaired functioning or <a href="/wiki/Distress_(medicine)" title="Distress (medicine)">distress</a> as a result of their gender identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM-IV-TR_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSM-IV-TR-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2016)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Transgender#Evolution_of_transgender_terminology" title="Transgender">Transgender § Evolution of transgender terminology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_use_of_transsexual">Origin and use of <i>transsexual</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origin and use of transsexual">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Norman_Haire" title="Norman Haire">Norman Haire</a> reported that in 1921,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Dora R of <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> began a surgical transition, under the care of <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Hirschfeld" title="Magnus Hirschfeld">Magnus Hirschfeld</a>, which ended in 1930 with a successful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genital_reassignment_surgery" title="Genital reassignment surgery">genital reassignment surgery</a>. In 1930, Hirschfeld supervised the second genital reassignment surgery to be reported in detail in a peer-reviewed journal, that of <a href="/wiki/Lili_Elbe" title="Lili Elbe">Lili Elbe</a> of <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>. In 1923, Hirschfeld introduced the (German) term "Transsexualismus",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> after which <a href="/wiki/David_Oliver_Cauldwell" title="David Oliver Cauldwell">David Oliver Cauldwell</a> introduced "transsexualism" and "transsexual" to English in 1949 and 1950.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cauldwell appears to be the first to use the term to refer to those who desired a change of physiological sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyerowitz_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-meyerowitz-14">[14]</a></sup> In 1969, <a href="/wiki/Harry_Benjamin" title="Harry Benjamin">Harry Benjamin</a> claimed to have been the first to use the term "transsexual" in a public lecture, which he gave in December 1953.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Benjamin went on to popularize the term in his 1966 book, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Transsexual_Phenomenon" title="The Transsexual Phenomenon">The Transsexual Phenomenon</a></i>, in which he described transsexual people on a scale (later called the "<a href="/wiki/Benjamin_scale" title="Benjamin scale">Benjamin scale</a>") of three levels of intensity: "Transsexual (nonsurgical)", "Transsexual (moderate intensity)", and "Transsexual (high intensity)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benjaminscale_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-benjaminscale-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ts-Si_Gaughan_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ts-Si_Gaughan-18">[18]</a></sup> In his book, Benjamin described "true" transsexualism as the following:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>True transsexuals feel that they belong to the other sex, they want to be and function as members of the opposite sex, not only to appear as such. For them, their sex organs, the primary (testes) as well as the secondary (penis and others) are disgusting deformities that must be changed by the surgeon's knife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Benjamin suggested that moderate intensity male to female transsexual people may benefit from estrogen medication as a "substitute for or preliminary to operation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benjaminscale_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-benjaminscale-16">[16]</a></sup> Some people have had SRS but do not meet the above definition of transsexual (e.g. Gregory Hemingway).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hemingway1_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hemingway1-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hemingway2_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hemingway2-21">[21]</a></sup> Other people do not desire SRS although they meet the other element's of Benjamin's definition of a "true transsexual".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nonopexample1_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nonopexample1-22">[22]</a></sup> Transexuality was included for the first time in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DSM-III" title="DSM-III">DSM-III</a> in 1980 and again in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DSM-III-R" title="DSM-III-R">DSM-III-R</a> in 1987, where it was located under <i>Disorders Usually First Evident in Infancy, Childhood or Adolescence</i>.</p>
<p>Beyond Benjamin's work, which focused on male-to-female transsexual people, there are cases of the female to male transsexual, for whom genital surgery may not be practical. Benjamin gave certifying letters to his MTF transsexual patients that stated "Their anatomical sex, that is to say, the body, is male. Their psychological sex, that is to say, the mind, is female." After 1967 Benjamin abandoned his early terminology and adopted that of "gender identity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ekins2006_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-ekins2006-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_of_transsexual_to_transgender">Relationship of <i>transsexual</i> to <i>transgender</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Relationship of transsexual to transgender">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Around the same time as Benjamin's book, in 1965, the term <i>transgender</i> was coined by John Oliven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bevan_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bevan-2">[2]</a></sup> By the 1990s, <i>transsexual</i> had come to be considered a subset of the <a href="/wiki/Umbrella_term" title="Umbrella term">umbrella term</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Transgender" title="Transgender">transgender</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TR2006_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-TR2006-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bevan_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bevan-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alegria_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alegria-3">[3]</a></sup> The term <i>transgender</i> is now more common, and many transgender people prefer the designation <i>transgender</i> and reject <i>transsexual</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polly_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polly-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swanson_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swanson-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GLAAD_Media_Reference_Guide_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-GLAAD_Media_Reference_Guide-26">[26]</a></sup> The term <i>transsexual</i>, however, continues to be used,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pauly_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pauly-27">[27]</a></sup> and some people who pursue medical assistance (for example, <a href="/wiki/Sex_reassignment_surgery" title="Sex reassignment surgery">sex reassignment surgery</a>) to change their sexual characteristics to match their gender identity prefer the designation <i>transsexual</i> and reject <i>transgender</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polly_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Polly-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swanson_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Swanson-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GLAAD_Media_Reference_Guide_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-GLAAD_Media_Reference_Guide-26">[26]</a></sup> One perspective offered by transsexual people who reject a transgender label for that of <i>transsexed</i> is that, for people who have gone through sexual reassignment surgery, their anatomical sex has been altered, whilst their gender remains constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historically, one reason some people preferred <i>transsexual</i> to <i>transgender</i> is that the medical community in the 1950s through the 1980s encouraged a distinction between the terms that would only allow the former access to medical treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-denny_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-denny-31">[31]</a></sup> Other self-identified transsexual people state that those who do not seek sex reassignment surgery (SRS) are fundamentally different from those who do, and that the two have different concerns,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ts-Si_Gaughan_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ts-Si_Gaughan-18">[18]</a></sup> but this view is controversial, and others argue that merely having some medical procedures does not have such far-reaching consequences as to put those who have them and those who have not (e.g. because they cannot afford them) into such distinctive categories. Another reason for objecting to the term <i>transsexual</i> is the concern that it implies something to do with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexuality" title="Sexuality">sexuality</a>, when it is actually about <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenway_Health_Glossary_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fenway_Health_Glossary-32">[32]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Christine_Jorgensen" title="Christine Jorgensen">Christine Jorgensen</a>, the first widely known person to have sex reassignment surgery (in this case, <a href="/wiki/Sex_reassignment_surgery_(male-to-female)" title="Sex reassignment surgery (male-to-female)">male-to-female</a>), rejected <i>transsexual</i> and instead identified herself in newsprint as <i>trans-gender</i>, on this basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminological_variance">Terminological variance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Terminological variance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word <i>transsexual</i> is most often used as an adjective rather than a noun – a "transsexual person" rather than simply "a transsexual". Like other trans people, transsexual people prefer to be referred to by the gender pronouns and terms associated with their gender identity. For example, a trans man is a person who was assigned the female sex at birth on the basis of his <a href="/wiki/Sex_organ" title="Sex organ">genitals</a> but, despite that assignment, identifies as a man and is transitioning or has transitioned to a male gender role; in the case of a <i>transsexual</i> man, he furthermore has or will have a masculine body. Transsexual people are sometimes referred to with directional terms, such as "female-to-male" for a transsexual man, abbreviated to "F2M", "FTM", and "F to M", or "male-to-female" for a transsexual woman, abbreviated "M2F", "MTF" and "M to F".</p>
<p>Individuals who have undergone and completed sex reassignment surgery are sometimes referred to as <i>transsexed</i> individuals;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> however, the term <i>transsexed</i> is not to be confused with the term <i>transexual</i>, which can also refer to individuals who have not yet undergone SRS, and whose anatomical sex (still) does not match their psychological sense of personal gender identity.</p>
<p>The terms <i>gender dysphoria</i> and <i>gender identity disorder</i> were not used until the 1970s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pauly_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pauly-27">[27]</a></sup> when Laub and Fisk published several works on transsexualism using these terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laub_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laub-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fisk_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fisk-37">[37]</a></sup> "Transsexualism" was replaced in the DSM-IV by "gender identity disorder in adolescents and adults".</p>
<p>Male-to-female transsexualism has sometimes been called "Harry Benjamin's syndrome" after the endocrinologist who pioneered the study of dysphoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> As the present-day medical study of gender variance is much broader than Benjamin's early description, there is greater understanding of its aspects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ekins2006_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-ekins2006-23">[23]</a></sup> and use of the term <i>Harry Benjamin's syndrome</i> has been criticized for delegitimizing gender-variant people with different experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Androphilia_and_gynephilia"><i>Androphilia</i> and <i>gynephilia</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Androphilia and gynephilia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Androphilia_and_gynephilia" title="Androphilia and gynephilia">Androphilia and gynephilia</a></div>
<p>The use of <i>homosexual transsexual</i> and related terms have been applied to transgender people since the middle of the 20th century, though concerns about the terms have been voiced since then. <a href="/wiki/Harry_Benjamin" title="Harry Benjamin">Harry Benjamin</a> said in 1966:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>...it seems evident that the question "Is the transsexual homosexual?" must be answered "yes" and " no." "Yes," if his anatomy is considered; "no" if his psyche is given preference.</p>
<p>What would be the situation after corrective surgery has been performed and the sex anatomy now resembles that of a woman? Is the "new woman" still a homosexual man? "Yes," if pedantry and technicalities prevail. "No" if reason and common sense are applied and if the respective patient is treated as an individual and not as a rubber stamp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benjamin1966_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-benjamin1966-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Many sources, including some supporters of the typology, criticize this choice of wording as confusing and degrading. Biologist <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Bagemihl" title="Bruce Bagemihl">Bruce Bagemihl</a> writes "..the point of reference for "heterosexual" or "homosexual" orientation in this nomenclature is solely the individual's genetic sex prior to reassignment (see for example, Blanchard et al. 1987[24], Coleman and Bockting, 1988[25], Blanchard, 1989[26]). These labels thereby ignore the individual’s personal sense of gender identity taking precedence over biological sex, rather than the other way around."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bagemihl_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-bagemihl-42">[42]</a></sup> Bagemihl goes on to take issue with the way this terminology makes it easy to claim transsexuals are really homosexual males seeking to escape from stigma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bagemihl_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-bagemihl-42">[42]</a></sup> Leavitt and Berger stated in 1990 that "The homosexual transsexual label is both confusing and controversial among males seeking sex reassignment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leavitt1990_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-leavitt1990-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morgan1978_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-morgan1978-44">[44]</a></sup> Critics argue that the term "homosexual transsexual" is "<a href="/wiki/Heterosexism" title="Heterosexism">heterosexist</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bagemihl_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-bagemihl-42">[42]</a></sup> "archaic",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wahng_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-wahng-45">[45]</a></sup> and demeaning because it labels people by sex assigned at birth instead of their <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leiblum2000_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-leiblum2000-46">[46]</a></sup> Benjamin, Leavitt, and Berger have all used the term in their own work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benjamin1966_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-benjamin1966-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leavitt1990_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-leavitt1990-43">[43]</a></sup> Sexologist <a href="/wiki/John_Bancroft_(sexologist)" title="John Bancroft (sexologist)">John Bancroft</a> also recently expressed regret for having used this terminology, which was standard when he used it, to refer to transsexual women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bancroftcomment_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bancroftcomment-47">[47]</a></sup> He says that he now tries to choose his words more sensitively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bancroftcomment_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bancroftcomment-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bancroftcomment_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bancroftcomment-47">[47]</a></sup> Sexologist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_Allen_Moser&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Charles Allen Moser (page does not exist)">Charles Allen Moser</a> is likewise critical of the terminology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moser2010_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-moser2010-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Use of <i>androphilia</i> and <i>gynephilia</i> was proposed and popularized by psychologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ron_Langevin" title="Ron Langevin">Ron Langevin</a> in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langevin1983_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-langevin1983-49">[49]</a></sup> Psychologist <a href="/wiki/Stephen_T._Wegener" title="Stephen T. Wegener">Stephen T. Wegener</a> writes, "Langevin makes several concrete suggestions regarding the language used to describe sexual anomalies. For example, he proposes the terms <i>gynephilic</i> and <i>androphilic</i> to indicate the type of partner preferred regardless of an individual's <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a> or dress. Those who are writing and researching in this area would do well to adopt his clear and concise vocabulary."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wegener_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-wegener-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Psychiatrist <a href="/wiki/Anil_Aggrawal" title="Anil Aggrawal">Anil Aggrawal</a> explains why the terms are useful in a glossary:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Androphilia – The romantic and/or sexual attraction to adult males. The term, along with gynephilia, is needed to overcome immense difficulties in characterizing the sexual orientation of transmen and transwomen. For instance, it is difficult to decide whether a transman erotically attracted to males is a heterosexual female or a homosexual male; or a transwoman erotically attracted to females is a heterosexual male or a lesbian female. Any attempt to classify them may not only cause confusion but arouse offense among the affected subjects. In such cases, while defining sexual attraction, it is best to focus on the object of their attraction rather than on the sex or gender of the subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Psychologist Rachel Ann Heath writes, "The terms homosexual and heterosexual are awkward, especially when the former is used with, or instead of, gay and lesbian. Alternatively, I use gynephilic and androphilic to refer to sexual preference for women and men, respectively. Gynephilic and androphilic derive from the Greek meaning love of a woman and love of a man respectively. So a gynephilic man is a man who likes women, that is, a heterosexual man, whereas an androphilic man is a man who likes men, that is, a gay man. For completeness, a lesbian is a gynephilic woman, a woman who likes other women. Gynephilic transsexed woman refers to a woman of transsexual background whose sexual preference is for women. Unless homosexual and heterosexual are more readily understood terms in a given context, this more precise terminology will be used throughout the book. Since homosexual, gay, and lesbian are often associated with bigotry and exclusion in many societies, the emphasis on sexual affiliation is both appropriate and socially just."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heath2006_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-heath2006-52">[52]</a></sup> Author <a href="/wiki/Helen_Boyd" title="Helen Boyd">Helen Boyd</a> agrees, writing, "It would be much more accurate to define sexual orientation as either "androphilic" (loving men) and "gynephilic" (loving women) instead."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boyd2007_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-boyd2007-53">[53]</a></sup> Sociomedical scientist Rebecca Jordan-Young challenges researchers like <a href="/wiki/Simon_LeVay" title="Simon LeVay">Simon LeVay</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._Michael_Bailey" title="J. Michael Bailey">J. Michael Bailey</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Lalumiere&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Martin Lalumiere (page does not exist)">Martin Lalumiere</a>, who she says "have completely failed to appreciate the implications of alternative ways of framing sexual orientation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jordan-young_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-jordan-young-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_diagnosis">Medical diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Medical diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Transsexualism appears in the <a href="/wiki/International_Statistical_Classification_of_Diseases_and_Related_Health_Problems" title="International Statistical Classification of Diseases and Related Health Problems">International Statistical Classification of Diseases and Related Health Problems</a> (ICD, currently in its <a href="/wiki/ICD-10" title="ICD-10">tenth edition</a>). The ICD-10 incorporates <i>transsexualism</i>, <i>dual role transvestism,</i> and <i>gender identity disorder of childhood</i> into its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gender_identity_disorder" title="Gender identity disorder">gender identity disorder</a> category. It defines transsexualism as "[a] desire to live and be accepted as a member of the opposite sex, usually accompanied by a sense of discomfort with, or inappropriateness of, one's anatomic sex, and a wish to have surgery and hormonal treatment to make one's body as congruent as possible with one's preferred sex."</p>
<p>Historically, transsexualism has also been included in the <a href="/wiki/American_Psychiatric_Association" title="American Psychiatric Association">American Psychiatric Association</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_and_Statistical_Manual_of_Mental_Disorders" title="Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders">Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders</a></i> (DSM). With the <a href="/wiki/DSM-5" title="DSM-5">DSM-5</a>, transsexualism was removed as a diagnosis, and a diagnosis of <a href="/wiki/Gender_dysphoria" title="Gender dysphoria">gender dysphoria</a> was created in its place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM-V_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSM-V-55">[55]</a></sup> This change was made to reflect the consensus view by members of the APA that transsexuality is not in and of itself a disorder and that transsexual people should not be stigmatized unnecessarily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> By including a diagnosis for gender dysphoria, transsexual people are still able to access medical care through the process of transition.</p>
<p>Similarly, the World Professional Association for Transgender Health (WPATH), and many transsexual people, have recommended the removal of transsexualism from the <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental health</a> chapter of the upcoming ICD, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICD-11" title="ICD-11">ICD-11</a> (due to be released in 2017).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> They argue that at least some mental health professionals are being insensitive by labelling transsexualism as "a disease", rather than the inborn trait which those who have it believe it to be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Principle 18 of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Yogyakarta_Principles" title="The Yogyakarta Principles">The Yogyakarta Principles</a>, a document of <a href="/wiki/International_human_rights_law" title="International human rights law">international human rights law</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> opposes such diagnosis as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_illness" title="Mental illness">mental illness</a> as medical abuse.</p>
<p>The current diagnosis for transsexual people who present themselves for medical treatment is gender dysphoria (leaving out those who have sexual identity disorders without gender concerns).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM-V_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-DSM-V-55">[55]</a></sup> According to the <i>Standards of care</i> formulated by the World Professional Association for Transgender Health (WPATH),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPATH_web_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-WPATH_web-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPATHSOCs_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-WPATHSOCs-61">[61]</a></sup> formerly the Harry Benjamin International Gender Dysphoria Association, this diagnostic label is often necessary to obtain sex reassignment therapy with health insurance coverage, and the designation of gender identity disorders as mental disorders is not a license for stigmatization or for the deprivation of gender patients' civil rights.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes.2C_studies.2C_and_theories">Causes, studies, and theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Causes, studies, and theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle selfreference noprint" role="note">This section is <a href="/wiki/Transclusion" title="Transclusion">transcluded</a> from <a href="/wiki/Causes_of_transsexuality" title="Causes of transsexuality">Causes of transsexuality</a>. <span class="plainlinks" style="font-style: normal"><small>(<a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Causes_of_transsexuality&amp;action=edit">edit</a> | <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Causes_of_transsexuality&amp;action=history">history</a>)</small></span></div>
<p>The causes of transsexuality have been studied for decades.</p>
<p>The most studied factors are biological. Studies beginning with Zhou in 1995 have found that trans women's brain structure (volume and density of neurons) is similar to <a href="/wiki/Cisgender" title="Cisgender">cisgender</a> (cis) women's and unlike cis men's, and trans men's is similar to cis men's, even when controlling for hormone use. A 2002 study by Chung suggested significant sexual dimorphism in the <a href="/wiki/Stria_terminalis#Bed_nucleus_of_the_stria_terminalis_.28BNST.29" title="Stria terminalis">BSTc</a> did not become established until adulthood, theorizing that either changes in fetal hormone levels produce changes in BSTc synaptic density and other factors which later lead to the observed differences in BSTc, or the differences are affected by the generation of a gender identity inconsistent with assigned sex. Studies in 2004 (Swaab), 2006 (Gooran), 2008 (Garcia-Falgueras), and 2010 (Rametti) confirmed earlier research finding that gender identity is influenced by brain structure. (Brain structure differences have also been noted between <a href="/wiki/Gay" title="Gay">gay</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterosexual" title="Heterosexual">heterosexual</a> men, and <a href="/wiki/Lesbian" title="Lesbian">lesbian</a> and heterosexual women as part of extensive research on <a href="/wiki/Biology_and_sexual_orientation" title="Biology and sexual orientation">biology and sexual orientation</a>.) Studies have also found that both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Androphilic_and_gynephilic" title="Androphilic and gynephilic">androphilic and gynephilic</a> trans women's brain function and responses are like cis women's and unlike cis men's, or are intermediate between the two. Likewise, studies such as Rametti's have found that trans men have male-like white matter patterns (even before using hormones), regardless of sexual orientation.</p>
<p>With regard to genetic factors, a study by Hare reported that trans women have a longer androgen receptor gene than cis men, which is less effective at binding testosterone, potentially preventing complete masculinization of the brain. (Prenatal androgen exposure or sensitivity, or lack thereof, is an often cited mechanism to explain observed brain-structure differences.) A study by Bentz found that trans men have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CYP17" title="CYP17">CYP17</a> allele distribution like cis men and unlike cis women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bentz_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bentz-62">[62]</a></sup> A twin study found that 33% of pairs of identical twins were both trans, compared to only 2.6% of non-identical twins who were raised in the same family at the same time, but were not genetically identical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TwinH_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-TwinH-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Psychological factors have also been proposed. The failure of an attempt to raise <a href="/wiki/David_Reimer" title="David Reimer">David Reimer</a> from infancy through adolescence as a girl after his genitals were accidentally mutilated is cited as disproving the theory that gender identity is determined by parenting. <a href="/wiki/Ray_Blanchard" title="Ray Blanchard">Ray Blanchard</a> developed <a href="/wiki/Blanchard%27s_transsexualism_typology" title="Blanchard's transsexualism typology">a taxonomy of male-to-female transsexuality</a> that assumes transsexuals are either homosexual or autogynephilic, but it is controversial; <a href="/wiki/J._Michael_Bailey" title="J. Michael Bailey">J. Michael Bailey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anne_Lawrence" title="Anne Lawrence">Anne Lawrence</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Cantor" title="James Cantor">James Cantor</a> and others support it, but Charles Allen Moser, Larry Nuttbrock, <a href="/wiki/Julia_Serano" title="Julia Serano">Julia Serano</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Professional_Association_for_Transgender_Health" title="World Professional Association for Transgender Health">World Professional Association for Transgender Health</a> and others argue it is poorly representative, non-instructive, and lacks empirical evidence, because the experiments behind it are poorly controlled and/or contradicted by other data.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_assistance">Medical assistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Medical assistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sex_reassignment_therapy">Sex reassignment therapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sex reassignment therapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sex_reassignment_therapy" title="Sex reassignment therapy">Sex reassignment therapy</a></div>
<p><i>Sex reassignment therapy</i> (SRT) is an umbrella term for all medical treatments related to sex reassignment of both transgender and <a href="/wiki/Intersex" title="Intersex">intersex</a> people. Though SRT is sometimes called "gender reassignment", those who use the word <i>sex</i> to describe an individual's biology, and <i>gender</i> to describe their personal identity and social role, consider this usage to be misleading.</p>
<p>Individuals make different choices regarding sex reassignment therapy, which may include <a href="/wiki/Hormone_replacement_therapy_(female-to-male)" title="Hormone replacement therapy (female-to-male)">female-to-male</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hormone_replacement_therapy_(male-to-female)" title="Hormone replacement therapy (male-to-female)">male-to-female</a> <a href="/wiki/Hormone_replacement_therapy" title="Hormone replacement therapy">hormone replacement therapy</a> (HRT) to modify <a href="/wiki/Secondary_sex_characteristic" title="Secondary sex characteristic">secondary sex characteristics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sex_reassignment_surgery" title="Sex reassignment surgery">sex reassignment surgery</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Orchiectomy" title="Orchiectomy">orchiectomy</a>) to alter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_sex_characteristics" title="Primary sex characteristics">primary sex characteristics</a>, chest surgery such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breast_augmentation_surgery" title="Breast augmentation surgery">top surgery</a> or <a href="/wiki/Breast_augmentation" title="Breast augmentation">breast augmentation</a>, or, in the case of trans women, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trachea_shave" title="Trachea shave">trachea shave</a>, <a href="/wiki/Facial_feminization_surgery" title="Facial feminization surgery">facial feminization surgery</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epilation" title="Epilation">permanent hair removal</a>.</p>
<p>To obtain sex reassignment therapy, transsexual people are generally required to undergo a psychological evaluation and receive a diagnosis of gender identity disorder in accordance with the Standards of Care (SOC) as published by the World Professional Association for Transgender Health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPATH_web_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-WPATH_web-60">[60]</a></sup> This assessment is usually accompanied by counseling on issues of adjustment to the desired gender role, effects and risks of medical treatments, and sometimes also by psychological therapy. The SOC are intended as guidelines, not inflexible rules, and are intended to ensure that clients are properly informed and in sound psychological health, and to discourage people from <a href="/wiki/Transitioning_(transgender)" title="Transitioning (transgender)">transitioning</a> based on unrealistic expectations.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_roles_and_transitioning">Gender roles and transitioning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Gender roles and transitioning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After an initial psychological evaluation, trans men and trans women may begin medical treatment, starting with hormone replacement therapy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPATHSOCs_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-WPATHSOCs-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gooren_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gooren-64">[64]</a></sup> or hormone blockers. In these cases, people who change their gender are usually required to live as members of their target gender for at least one year prior to genital surgery, gaining <a href="/wiki/Real-life_experience_(transgender)" title="Real-life experience (transgender)">real-life experience</a>, which is sometimes called the "real-life test" (RLT).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPATHSOCs_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-WPATHSOCs-61">[61]</a></sup> Transsexual individuals may undergo some, all, or none of the medical procedures available, depending on personal feelings, health, income, and other considerations. Some people posit that transsexualism is a physical condition, not a psychological issue, and assert that sex reassignment therapy should be given on request. (Brown 103)</p>
<p>Like other trans people, transsexual people may refer to themselves as trans men or trans women. Transsexual people desire to establish a permanent gender role as a member of the gender with which they identify, and many transsexual people pursue medical interventions as part of the process of expressing their gender. The entire process of switching from one physical sex and social gender presentation to another is often referred to as transitioning, and usually takes several years. Transsexual people who transition usually change their social gender roles, <a href="/wiki/Legal_name" title="Legal name">legal names</a> and legal sex designation.</p>
<p>Not all transsexual people undergo a physical transition. Some find reasons not to; for example, the expense of surgery, the risk of medical complications, or medical conditions which make the use of hormones or surgery dangerous. Some may not identify strongly with another binary gender role. Others may find balance at a mid-point during the process, regardless of whether or not they are binary-identified. Many transsexual people, including binary-identified transsexual people, do not undergo genital surgery, because they are comfortable with their own genitals, or because they are concerned about nerve damage and the potential loss of sexual pleasure, including orgasm. This is especially so in the case of trans men, many of whom are dissatisfied with the current state of <a href="/wiki/Phalloplasty" title="Phalloplasty">phalloplasty</a>, which is typically very expensive, not covered by health insurance, and commonly does not achieve desired results. For example, not only does phalloplasty not result in a completely natural erection, it may not allow for an erection at all, and its results commonly lack penile sexual sensitivity; in other cases, however, phalloplasty results are satisfying for trans men. By contrast, <a href="/wiki/Metoidioplasty" title="Metoidioplasty">metoidioplasty</a>, which is more popular, is significantly less expensive and has far better sexual results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teich_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Teich-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stryker_.26_Whittle_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stryker_.26_Whittle-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some transsexual people live heterosexual lifestyles (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heteronormative" title="Heteronormative">heteronormative</a>), while some identify as gay, lesbian,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ekins2006_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-ekins2006-23">[23]</a></sup> or bisexual. Many transsexual people choose the language of how they refer to their sexual orientation based on their gender identity, not their birth <a href="/wiki/Sex_assignment" title="Sex assignment">assigned sex</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ekins2006_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-ekins2006-23">[23]</a></sup> though some transsexual people still find identification with a physical-sex-based community: many trans men, for instance, are involved with lesbian communities.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychological_treatment">Psychological treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Psychological treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Psychological techniques that attempt to alter gender identity to one considered appropriate for the person's assigned sex are typically ineffective. The widely recognized Standards of Care<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPATHSOCs_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-WPATHSOCs-61">[61]</a></sup> note that sometimes the only reasonable and effective course of treatment for transsexual people is to go through sex reassignment therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPATHSOCs_61-4"><a href="#cite_note-WPATHSOCs-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>The need for treatment of transsexual people is emphasized by the high rate of <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental health</a> problems, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" title="Clinical depression">depression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, and various <a href="/wiki/Substance_dependence" title="Substance dependence">addictions</a>, as well as a higher <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a> rate among untreated transsexual people than in the general population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> These problems are alleviated by a change of gender role and/or physical characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many transgender and transsexual activists, and many caregivers, note that these problems are not usually related to the gender identity issues themselves, but the social and cultural responses to gender-variant individuals. Some transsexual people reject the counseling that is recommended by the Standards of Care<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPATHSOCs_61-5"><a href="#cite_note-WPATHSOCs-61">[61]</a></sup> because they do not consider their gender identity to be a cause of psychological problems.</p>
<p>Brown and Rounsley<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> noted that "[s]ome transsexual people acquiesce to legal and medical expectations in order to gain rights granted through the medical/psychological hierarchy." Legal needs such as a change of sex on legal documents, and medical needs, such as sex reassignment surgery, are usually difficult to obtain without a doctor or therapist's approval. Because of this, some transsexual people feel coerced into affirming outdated concepts of gender to overcome simple legal and medical hurdles (Brown 107).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regrets_and_detransitions">Regrets and detransitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Regrets and detransitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>People who undergo sex reassignment surgery can develop regret for the procedure later in life, largely due to lack of support from family or peers, with data from the 1990s suggesting a rate of 3.8%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In a 2001 study of 232 MTF patients who underwent GRS with Dr. Toby Meltzer, none of the patients reported complete regret and only 6% reported partial or occasional regrets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrence_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrence-74">[74]</a></sup> A 2009 review of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medline" title="Medline">Medline</a> literature suggests the total rate of patients expressing feelings of doubt or regret is estimated to be as high as 8%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> An issue reported by some is the inability to find sexual partners.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>A 2010 meta-study, based on 28 previous long-term studies of transsexual men and women, found that the overall psychological functioning of transsexual people after transition was similar to that of the general population and significantly better than that of untreated transsexual people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevalence">Prevalence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Prevalence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Estimates of the <a href="/wiki/Prevalence" title="Prevalence">prevalence</a> of transgender people are highly dependent on the specific case definitions used in the studies, with prevalence rates varying by orders of magnitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jsm.jsexmed.org_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-jsm.jsexmed.org-77">[77]</a></sup> The most frequently quoted estimate of prevalence<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (March 2016)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> is from the Amsterdam Gender Dysphoria Clinic;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> over four decades the clinic has treated roughly 95% of Dutch transsexual clients, and it suggests a prevalence of 1:10,000 among assigned males and 1:30,000 among assigned females. In the US, the DSM-IV (1994) says that roughly 1 in 30,000 assigned males and 1 in 100,000 assigned females seek <a href="/wiki/Sex_reassignment_surgery" title="Sex reassignment surgery">sex reassignment surgery</a>. Though no direct studies on the prevalence of GID have been done, a variety of clinical papers published in the past 20 years provide estimates ranging from 1:7,400 to 1:42,000 in assigned males and 1:30,040 to 1:104,000 in assigned females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> A 2008 study of the number of New Zealand passport holders who changed the sex on their passport estimated that 1:3,639 birth-assigned males and 1:22,714 birth-assigned females were transsexual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-veale2_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-veale2-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most recent (2016) systematic review of prevalence, leading to a meta-analysis of 27 studies, found estimates per 100,000 population of 9.2 (95% CI = 4.9–13.6) for surgical or hormonal gender affirmation therapy and 6.8 (95% CI = 4.6–9.1) for transgender-related diagnoses. Of studies assessing self-reported transgender identity, prevalence was 871 (95% CI = 519–1,224); however, this result was influenced by a single outlier study. After removal of that study, the figure dropped to 355 (95% CI = 144–566). Significant heterogeneity was observed in most analyses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jsm.jsexmed.org_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-jsm.jsexmed.org-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Olyslager and <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Conway" title="Lynn Conway">Conway</a> presented a paper<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> at the WPATH 20th International Symposium (2007) arguing that the data from their own and other studies actually imply much higher prevalence, with minimum lower bounds of 1:4,500 male-to-female transsexual people and 1:8,000 female-to-male transsexual people for a number of countries worldwide. They estimate the number of post-op women in the US to be 32,000 and obtain a figure of 1:2500 male-to-female transsexual people. They further compare the annual incidences of SRS and male birth in the U.S. to obtain a figure of 1:1000 MTF transsexual people and suggest a prevalence of 1:500 extrapolated from the rising rates of SRS in the US and a "common sense" estimate of the number of undiagnosed transsexual people. Olyslager and Conway also argue that the US population of assigned males having already undergone reassignment surgery by the top three US SRS surgeons alone is enough to account for the entire transsexual population implied by the 1:10,000 prevalence number, yet this excludes all other US SRS surgeons, surgeons in countries such as Thailand, Canada, and others, and the high proportion of transsexual people who have not yet sought treatment, suggesting that a prevalance of 1:10,000 is too low.</p>
<p>A study of Swedes estimated a ratio of 1.4:1 trans women to trans men for those requesting sex reassignment surgery and a ratio of 1:1 for those who proceeded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sept2207_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-sept2207-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>A presentation at the LGBT Health Summit in Bristol, UK,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> based upon figures from a number of reputable European and UK sources, shows that this population is increasing rapidly (14% per year) and that the mean age of <a href="/wiki/Transitioning_(transgender)" title="Transitioning (transgender)">transition</a> is actually rising.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_orientation">Sexual orientation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sexual orientation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2008, a National Transgender Discrimination Survey was conducted by the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Transgender_Equality" title="National Center for Transgender Equality">National Center for Transgender Equality</a> and the National LGBTQ Task Force. Of 6436 transgender people responding, 21% were homosexuals, 23% bisexuals, 21% heterosexuals, 20% queers, 4% asexuals, and 11% others. Of them 26% were men, 41% women, 13% others, and 20% varied. At birth, 60% were assessed as men, 40% as women, 76% were white and 11% multiracial. Of respondents, 46% identified strongly in "male-to-female", 26% in "female-to-male", 46% to "transsexual", 65% to "transgender", 32% to "gender non-conforming, 15% to "cross-dresser", etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_transgender_people" title="List of transgender people">List of transgender people</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Thailand#Trans_life_in_the_country" title="LGBT rights in Thailand">LGBT rights in Thailand § Trans life in the country</a></div>
<p>An apparent transsexual named <a href="/wiki/Elagabalus" title="Elagabalus">Elagabalus</a> was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Emperor" title="Roman Emperor">Roman Emperor</a> from 218 to 222.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glbtq-enc-elagabal_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-glbtq-enc-elagabal-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> and <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a> cultures have traditional social and ceremonial roles for individuals who do not fit into the usual roles for males and females in that culture. These roles can vary widely between tribes, because gender roles, when they exist at all, also vary considerably among different Native cultures. However, a modern, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pan-Indian" title="Pan-Indian">pan-Indian</a> status known as <a href="/wiki/Two-Spirit" title="Two-Spirit">Two-Spirit</a> has emerged among LGBT Natives in recent years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilley_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilley-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_and_social_aspects">Legal and social aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Legal and social aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_aspects_of_transsexualism" title="Legal aspects of transsexualism">Legal aspects of transsexualism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anna_Grodzka.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="645" data-file-width="565" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Anna_Grodzka.jpg/200px-Anna_Grodzka.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Anna_Grodzka.jpg/300px-Anna_Grodzka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Anna_Grodzka.jpg/400px-Anna_Grodzka.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anna_Grodzka.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Anna_Grodzka" title="Anna Grodzka">Anna Grodzka</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> is the first transsexual MP in the history of Europe to have had <a href="/wiki/Sex_reassignment_surgery" title="Sex reassignment surgery">sex reassignment surgery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Laws regarding changes to the legal status of transsexual people are different from country to country. Some jurisdictions allow an individual to <a href="/wiki/Name_change" title="Name change">change their name</a>, and sometimes, their legal gender, to reflect their gender identity. Within the US, some states allow amendments or complete replacement of the original birth certificates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transgenderlaw.org_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-transgenderlaw.org-90">[90]</a></sup> Some states seal earlier records against all but court orders in order to protect the transsexual person's privacy.</p>
<p>In many places, it is not possible to change birth records or other legal designations of sex, although changes are occurring. <a href="/wiki/Estelle_Asmodelle" title="Estelle Asmodelle">Estelle Asmodelle</a>’s book documented her struggle to change the Australian birth certificate and passport laws, although there are other individuals who have been instrumental in changing laws and thus attaining more acceptance for transsexual people in general.</p>
<p>Medical treatment for transsexual and transgender people is available in most Western countries. However, transsexual and transgender people challenge the "normative" gender roles of many cultures and often face considerable hatred and prejudice. The film <i><a href="/wiki/Boys_Don%27t_Cry_(film)" title="Boys Don't Cry (film)">Boys Don't Cry</a></i> chronicles the case of <a href="/wiki/Brandon_Teena" title="Brandon Teena">Brandon Teena</a>, a transsexual man who was raped and murdered after his status was discovered. The project <i>Remembering Our Dead</i>, founded by Gwendolyn Ann Smith, archives numerous cases of transsexual and transgender people being murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In the United States, November 20 has been set aside as the "Day of Remembrance" for all murdered transgender people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jurisdictions allowing changes to birth records generally allow trans people to marry members of the opposite sex to their gender identity and to adopt children. Jurisdictions which prohibit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Same_sex_marriage" title="Same sex marriage">same sex marriage</a> often require pre-transition marriages to be ended before they will issue an amended birth certificate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Health-practitioner manuals, professional journalistic <a href="/wiki/Style_guide" title="Style guide">style guides</a>, and LGBT advocacy groups advise the adoption by others of the name and pronouns identified by the person in question, including present references to the transgender or transsexual person's past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APA_Complicated_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-APA_Complicated-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenway_Meeting_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fenway_Meeting-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenway_Glossary_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fenway_Glossary-96">[96]</a></sup> Family members and friends who may be confused about pronoun usage or the definitions of <a href="/wiki/Sex" title="Sex">sex</a> are commonly instructed in proper pronoun usage, either by the transsexual person or by professionals or other persons familiar with pronoun usage as it relates to transsexual people. Sometimes transsexual people have to correct their friends and family members many times before they begin to use the transsexual person's desired pronouns consistently. According to <a href="/wiki/Julia_Serano" title="Julia Serano">Julia Serano</a>, deliberate mis-gendering of transsexual people is "an arrogant attempt to belittle and humiliate trans people"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Both "transsexualism" and "gender identity disorders not resulting from physical impairments" are specifically excluded from coverage under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Americans_with_Disabilities_Act" title="Americans with Disabilities Act">Americans with Disabilities Act</a> Section 12211.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Gender dysphoria is not excluded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Employment_issues">Employment issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Employment issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Openly transsexual people can have difficulty maintaining employment. Most find it necessary to remain employed during transition in order to cover the costs of living and transition. However, <a href="/wiki/Employment_discrimination" title="Employment discrimination">employment discrimination</a> against trans people is rampant and many of them are fired when they come out or are involuntarily <a href="/wiki/Outing" title="Outing">outed</a> at work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Transsexual people must decide whether to transition on-the-job,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> or to find a new job when they make their social transition. Other stresses that transsexual people face in the workplace are being fearful of coworkers negatively responding to their transition, and losing job experience under a previous name—even deciding which rest room to use can prove challenging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Finding employment can be especially challenging for those in mid-transition.</p>
<p>Laws regarding name and gender changes in many countries make it difficult for transsexual people to conceal their trans status from their employers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Because the Harry Benjamin Standards of Care requires one-year of real life experience prior to SRS, some feel this creates a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catch_22" title="Catch 22">Catch 22</a> situation which makes it difficult for trans people to remain employed or obtain SRS.</p>
<p>In many countries, laws provide protection from workplace discrimination based on gender identity or gender expression, including masculine women and feminine men. An increasing number of companies are including "gender identity and expression" in their non-discrimination policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transgenderlaw.org_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-transgenderlaw.org-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Often these laws and policies do not cover all situations and are not strictly enforced. <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Anti-discrimination_law" title="Anti-discrimination law">anti-discrimination laws</a> protect transsexual persons in the workplace and specifically prohibit employers from terminating or refusing to hire a person based on their transsexuality. The <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> provides employment protection as part of gender discrimination protections following the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">European Court of Justice</a> decisions in <i><a href="/wiki/P_v_S_and_Cornwall_County_Council" title="P v S and Cornwall County Council">P v S and Cornwall County Council</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States National Transgender Discrimination Survey, 44% of respondents reported not getting a job they applied for because of being transgender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endtransdiscrimination.org_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-endtransdiscrimination.org-106">[106]</a></sup> 36% of trans women reported losing a job due to discrimination compared to 19% of trans men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endtransdiscrimination.org_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-endtransdiscrimination.org-106">[106]</a></sup> 54% of trans women and 50% of trans men report having been harassed in the workplace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endtransdiscrimination.org_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-endtransdiscrimination.org-106">[106]</a></sup> Transgender people who have been fired due to bias are more than 34 times likely than members of the general population to attempt suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endtransdiscrimination.org_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-endtransdiscrimination.org-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stealth">Stealth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Stealth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many transsexual men and women choose to live completely as members of their gender without disclosing details of their birth-assigned sex. This approach is sometimes called <i>stealth.</i><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="true, but needs a source (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Stealthy transsexual people choose not to disclose their past for numerous reasons, including fear of discrimination and fear of physical violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="the term 'stealth' does not appear anywhere in the source; 2. transsexuals living in stealth mode are not 'stealthy' (July 2016)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> There are examples of people having been denied medical treatment upon discovery of their trans status, whether it was revealed by the patient or inadvertently discovered by the doctors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TyraTGR_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-TyraTGR-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_media">In the media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transsexual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: In the media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_transgender_characters_in_film_and_television" title="List of transgender characters in film and television">List of transgender characters in film and television</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nina_Poon_threefour_Shankbone_2010_NYC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2367" data-file-width="1830" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Nina_Poon_threefour_Shankbone_2010_NYC.jpg/220px-Nina_Poon_threefour_Shankbone_2010_NYC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Nina_Poon_threefour_Shankbone_2010_NYC.jpg/330px-Nina_Poon_threefour_Shankbone_2010_NYC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Nina_Poon_threefour_Shankbone_2010_NYC.jpg/440px-Nina_Poon_threefour_Shankbone_2010_NYC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nina_Poon_threefour_Shankbone_2010_NYC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nina Poon, a transsexual model who has appeared in <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Cole_Productions" title="Kenneth Cole Productions">Kenneth Cole</a> ads, at the 2010 <a href="/wiki/Tribeca_Film_Festival" title="Tribeca Film Festival">Tribeca Film Festival</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Transsexualism was discussed in the mass media as long ago as the 1930s. The American magazine <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_magazine" title="Time magazine">Time</a></i> in 1936 devoted an article to what it called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermaphrodites#Other_uses_of_the_term" title="Hermaphrodites">hermaphrodites</a>", treating the subject with sensitivity and not sensationalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> It described the call by <a href="/wiki/Avery_Brundage" title="Avery Brundage">Avery Brundage</a>, who led the American team to the <a href="/wiki/1936_Summer_Olympics" title="1936 Summer Olympics">1936 Summer Olympics</a> in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>, that a system be established to examine female athletes for "sex ambiguities"; two athletes changed sex after the Games.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christine_Jorgensen" title="Christine Jorgensen">Christine Jorgensen</a> was a transgender woman who received considerable attention in American mass media in the 1950s. Jorgensen was a former <a href="/wiki/G.I._(military)" title="G.I. (military)">G.I.</a> that went to Denmark to receive sex reassignment surgery. Her story appeared in publications including <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)"><i>Time</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a>. Other representations of transgender women appeared in mainstream media in the 1950s and 1960s, such as <a href="/wiki/Delisa_Newton" title="Delisa Newton">Delisa Newton</a>, Charlotte McLeod, Tamara Rees, and Marta Olmos Ramiro, but Jorgensen received the most attention. Her story was sensationalized, but received positively. In comparison, news articles about Newton, McLeod, Rees, and Ramiro had negative implications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before transsexual people were depicted in popular movies and television shows, <a href="/wiki/Aleshia_Brevard" title="Aleshia Brevard">Aleshia Brevard</a>—a transsexual whose surgery took place in 1962<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brevard-2011_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brevard-2011-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:3</sup>—was actively working as an actress<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brevard-2011_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brevard-2011-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:141</sup> and model<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brevard-2011_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brevard-2011-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:200</sup> in Hollywood and New York throughout the 1960s and '70s. Aleshia never portrayed a transsexual person, though she appeared in eight Hollywood produced films, on most of the popular variety shows of the day including <a href="/wiki/The_Dean_Martin_Show" title="The Dean Martin Show">The Dean Martin Show</a>, and was a regular on <a href="/wiki/The_Red_Skelton_Show" title="The Red Skelton Show">The Red Skelton Show</a> and <a href="/wiki/One_Life_to_Live" title="One Life to Live">One Life to Live</a> before returning to University to teach Drama and Acting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brevard-2011_111-3"><a href="#cite_note-Brevard-2011-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Harris" title="Thomas Harris">Thomas Harris</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Silence_of_the_Lambs_(novel)" title="The Silence of the Lambs (novel)">Silence of the Lambs</a></i> included a serial killer who considered himself a transsexual. After being turned down for sex reassignment surgery due to not meeting necessary psychological evaluations, he then harvested female bodies to make a feminine suit. In the novel, it is noted that the character is not actually a transsexual; this distinction is made only briefly in the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Films depicting transgender issues include: <i><a href="/wiki/Come_Back_to_the_Five_and_Dime,_Jimmy_Dean,_Jimmy_Dean_(film)" title="Come Back to the Five and Dime, Jimmy Dean, Jimmy Dean (film)">Come Back to the Five and Dime, Jimmy Dean, Jimmy Dean</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_According_to_Garp" title="The World According to Garp">The World According to Garp</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Priscilla,_Queen_of_the_Desert" title="The Adventures of Priscilla, Queen of the Desert">The Adventures of Priscilla, Queen of the Desert</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/All_About_My_Mother" title="All About My Mother">All About My Mother</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Crying_Game" title="The Crying Game">The Crying Game</a></i>. The film <i><a href="/wiki/Different_for_Girls" title="Different for Girls">Different for Girls</a></i> is notable for its depiction of a transsexual woman who meets up with, and forms a romantic relationship with, her former best friend from her all-male boarding school. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ma_Vie_en_Rose" title="Ma Vie en Rose">Ma Vie en Rose</a></i> portrays a six-year-old child who is <a href="/wiki/Gender_variance" title="Gender variance">gender variant</a>. The film <i><a href="/wiki/Wild_Zero" title="Wild Zero">Wild Zero</a></i> features Kwancharu Shitichai, a transsexual Thai actor. When the main character is conflicted about falling in love with a "woman who is also a man", <a href="/wiki/Guitar_Wolf" title="Guitar Wolf">Guitar Wolf</a> tells him "Love knows no race, nationality or gender!"</p>
<p>Although <i><a href="/wiki/Better_Than_Chocolate" title="Better Than Chocolate">Better Than Chocolate</a></i> is primarily about the romance of two lesbians, a subplot in the 1999 Canadian film has Judy (<a href="/wiki/Peter_Outerbridge" title="Peter Outerbridge">Peter Outerbridge</a>), a <a href="/wiki/Trans_woman" title="Trans woman">trans woman</a> with a crush on Frances (<a href="/wiki/Ann-Marie_MacDonald" title="Ann-Marie MacDonald">Ann-Marie MacDonald</a>), the owner of a lesbian bookstore. Within the film has a few scenes showing how Judy loses her parents who are unable to accept her, and buy her off with a bye forever present in purchasing a home for her.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Southern_Comfort_(2001_film)" title="Southern Comfort (2001 film)">Southern Comfort</a></i> is a 2001 documentary by filmmaker Katie Davis, which follows the final months of the life of Robert Eads, a female-to-male transsexual living in Georgia. Eads was diagnosed with ovarian cancer and rejected for treatment by over two dozen doctors due to his transsexuality. The documentary follows Eads and several of his closest friends, a support group of transsexual southerners known as "Southern Comfort". The documentary won several awards, including the Grand Jury Prize at the <a href="/wiki/Sundance_Film_Festival" title="Sundance Film Festival">Sundance Film Festival</a>, First Prize at the <a href="/wiki/Seattle_International_Film_Festival" title="Seattle International Film Festival">Seattle International Film Festival</a>, and the Special Audience Award at the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_International_Film_Festival" title="Berlin International Film Festival">Berlin International Film Festival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two notable films depict transphobic violence based on true events: <i><a href="/wiki/Soldier%27s_Girl" title="Soldier's Girl">Soldier's Girl</a></i> (about the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Barry_Winchell" title="Barry Winchell">Barry Winchell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calpernia_Addams" title="Calpernia Addams">Calpernia Addams</a>, and Winchell's subsequent murder) and <i><a href="/wiki/Boys_Don%27t_Cry_(film)" title="Boys Don't Cry (film)">Boys Don't Cry</a></i> (about <a href="/wiki/Brandon_Teena" title="Brandon Teena">Brandon Teena</a>'s murder). Calpernia Addams has appeared in numerous movies and television shows, including the 2005 movie <i><a href="/wiki/Transamerica_(film)" title="Transamerica (film)">Transamerica</a></i>, in which <a href="/wiki/Felicity_Huffman" title="Felicity Huffman">Felicity Huffman</a> portrays a transsexual woman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>In fall 2005, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sundance_Channel_(United_States)" title="Sundance Channel (United States)">Sundance Channel</a> aired a documentary series known as <i><a href="/wiki/TransGeneration" title="TransGeneration">TransGeneration</a></i>. This series focused on four transsexual college students, including two trans women and two trans men, in various stages of transition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> In February 2006, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logo_(TV_channel)" title="Logo (TV channel)">Logo</a> aired <i>Beautiful Daughters</i>, a documentary film about the first all-trans cast of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Vagina_Monologues" title="The Vagina Monologues">The Vagina Monologues</a></i>, which included Addams, <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Conway" title="Lynn Conway">Lynn Conway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrea_James" title="Andrea James">Andrea James</a>, and Leslie Townsend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Also in 2006, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lifetime_(TV_channel)" title="Lifetime (TV channel)">Lifetime</a> aired a movie biography on <a href="/wiki/Murder_of_Gwen_Araujo" title="Murder of Gwen Araujo">the murder of "Eddie"/"Gwen" Araujo</a> called <i><a href="/wiki/A_Girl_Like_Me:_The_Gwen_Araujo_Story" title="A Girl Like Me: The Gwen Araujo Story">A Girl Like Me: The Gwen Araujo Story</a></i>.</p>
<p>Transsexual people have also been depicted in popular television shows. In part of the first season of the 1970s t.v. comedy series, <i><a href="/wiki/Soap_(TV_series)" title="Soap (TV series)">Soap</a></i>, Billy Crystal plays <a href="/wiki/Jodie_Dallas" title="Jodie Dallas">Jodie Dallas</a>, a gay man who is about to undergo a sex change in order to legally marry his male lover, who breaks off the relationship just before the surgery.
